A typical IDA system consists of three core components:\n\n1.  **Host (A):** A molecule possessing a recognition cavity or binding site.\n2.  **Indicator (B):** A guest molecule that exhibits a signal response (e.g., in its color or fluorescence).\n3.  **Analyte (C):** The target guest molecule to be detected.\n\nThe principle of operation involves two steps:\n\n1. It binds to CB[8] via cation-π and hydrophobic interactions, resulting in a change in its optical properties (e.g., fluorescence quenching or color change). MB is water-soluble and small enough to fit inside CB[8].\n\n2. Requirements for the Unknown Component (Analyte C):\n\n- The analyte must be a guest molecule that binds to CB[8] with higher affinity than MB, displacing MB from the host cavity.\n- It should be cationic or have strong hydrophobic/aromatic character to maximize host-guest interactions with CB[8].\n- The analyte must be water-soluble and of appropriate size and shape to fit within the CB[8] cavity.\n- It should not be so large, rigid, or complex that it cannot enter the cavity, nor should it be a polymer, inorganic solid, or a molecule with poor aqueous solubility.\n\n3. A typical IDA system consists of three core components:\n\n1.  **Host (A):** A molecule possessing a recognition cavity or binding site.\n2.  **Indicator (B):** A guest molecule that exhibits a signal response (e.g., in its color or fluorescence).\n3.  **Analyte (C):** The target guest molecule to be detected.\n\nThe principle of operation involves two steps:\n\n1. This displacement restores the indicator\'s signal to its original state, thus enabling the detection of the analyte C.\n\n------\n\n**Problem Statement**\n\nA given IDA system consists of the following components:\n\n* **Known Component 1:** Cucurbit[8]uril (CB[8]): A large, barrel-shaped macrocyclic molecule composed of eight glycoluril units joined by sixteen methylene bridges.
